THE COUNTESS 
 of ALBANY 


 BY 
 VERNON LEE 

 to the memory of MY FRIEND 


 MADAME JOHN MEYER , 


 I dedicate this volume , 
 so OFTEN and so LATELY TALKED over together , 
 in GRATEFUL and AFFECTIONATE REGRET . 




 PREFACE 


 in prepare this volume on the Countess of Albany   which I consider as 
 a kind of completion of my previous study of eighteenth - century 
 Italy , I have avail myself largely of Baron Alfred von Reumont 's 
 large work die Gräfin von Albany   publish in 1862 ; and of the 
 monograph , itself partially found on the foregoing , of M. St. René 
 Taillandier , entitle La Comtesse d'Albany , publish in Paris in 
 1862 . Baron von Reumont 's two volume , write twenty year ago and when 
 the generation which have come into personal contact with the Countess of 
 Albany have not yet entirely die out ; and M. St. René Taillandier 's 
 volume , which embody the result of his research into the archive of 
 the Musée Fabre at Montpellier ; might naturally be expect to have 
 exhaust all the information obtainable about the subject of their and 
 my study . this have prove to be the case very much less than might 
 have be anticipate . the publication , by Jacopo Bernardi and Carlo 
 Milanesi , of a number of letter of Alfieri to sienese friend , have 
 afford I an insight into Alfieri 's character and his relation with 
 the Countess of Albany such as be unattainable to Baron von Reumont and 
 to M. St. René Taillandier . the examination , by myself and my friend 
 Signor Mario Pratesi , of several hundred of MS . letter of the Countess 
 of Albany exist in public and private archive at Siena and at 
 Milan , have add an important amount of what I may call psychological 
 detail , overlook by Baron von Reumont and unguesse by M. St. René 
 Taillandier . I have , therefore , I trust , be able to reconstruct the 
 Countess of Albany 's spiritual likeness during the period - that of she 
 early connection with Alfieri - which my predecessor have be satisfy 
 to despatch in comparatively few page , counterbalance the thinness of 
 this portion of their biography by a degree of detail concern the 
 Countess 's latter year , and the friend with whom she then correspond , 
 which , however interesting , can not be consider as vital to the real 
 subject of their work . 

 besides the volume of Baron von Reumont and M. St. René Taillandier , I 
 have depend mainly upon Alfieri 's autobiography , edit by Professor 
 Teza , and supplement by Bernardi 's and Milanesi 's Lettere di Vittorio 
 Alfieri , publish by Le Monnier in 1862 . among english book that I 
 have put under contribution , I may mention Klose 's Memoirs of Prince 
 Charles Edward Stuart   Colburn , 1845 , Ewald 's Life and Times of 
 Prince Charles Stuart   Chapman and Hall , 1875 , and Sir Horace Mann 's 
  letter to Walpole , edit by Dr. Doran . a review , variously 
 attribute to Lockhart and to Dennistoun , in the Quarterly for 1847 , 
 have be all the more useful to I as I have be unable to procure , 
 write in Italy , the Tales of the Century , of which that paper give 
 a masterly account . 

 for various detail I must refer to Charles Dutens ' Mémoires d'un 
 Voyageur qui se repose   Paris , 1806 ; to Silvagni 's La Corte e la 
 Societ   Romana nel secolo XVIII . ; to Foscolo 's Correspondence , Gino 
 Capponi 's Ricordi and those of d'Azeglio ; to Giordani 's work and 
 Benass   Montanari 's life of Ippolito Pindemonti , besides the book 
 quote by Baron Reumont ; and for what I may call the general pervading 
 historical colouring   if indeed I have succeed in give any   of the 
 background against which I have try to sketch the Countess of Albany , 
 Charles Edward and Alfieri , I can only refer generally to what be 
 now a vague mass of detail accumulate by myself during the year of 
 preparation for my Studies of the Eighteenth Century in Italy . 

 my debt to the kindness of person who have put unpublished matter at my 
 disposal , or help I to collect various information , be a large one . 
 in the first category , I wish to express my good thank to the Director 
 of the Public Library at Siena ; to cavaliere Guiseppe Porri , a great 
 collector of autograph , in the same city ; to the Countess Baldelli and 
 Cavaliere Emilio Santarelli of Florence , who possess some most curious 
 portrait and other relic of the Countess of Albany , Prince Charles 
 Edward , and Alfieri ; and also to my friend Count Pierre Boutourline , 
 whose grandfather and great - aunt be among Madame d'Albany 's friend . 
 among those who have kindly give I the benefit of their advice and 
 assistance , I must mention foremost my friend Signor Mario Pratesi , the 
 eminent novelist ; and next to he the learn Director of the State 
 Archives of Florence , Cavaliere Gaetano Milanese , and Doctor Guido 
 Biagi , of the Biblioteca Vittorio Emanuel of Rome , without whose 
 kindness my work would have be quite impossible . 

 Florence , 
  March 15 , 1884 . 

 CHAPTER I. 

 THE BRIDE . 


 on the Wednesday or Thursday of Holy Week of the year 1772 the inhabitant 
 of the squalid and dilapidate little mountain town between Ancona and 
 Loreto be throw into great excitement by the passage of a travel 
 equipage , doubtless follow by two or three dependent chaise , of more 
 than usual magnificence . 

 the people of those part have little to do now - a - day , and must have 
 have still less during the Pontificate of his Holiness Pope Clement XIV . ; 
 and we can imagine how all the window of the unplastered house , all 
 the black and oozy doorway , must have be line with head of woman 
 and child ; how the principal square of each town , where the horse 
 be change , must have be crowd with inquisitive townsfolk and 
 peasant , whispering , as they hang about the carriage , that the great 
 traveller be the young queen of England go to meet her bridegroom ; 
 a thing to be remember in such world - forget place as these , and 
 which must have furnish the subject of conversation for month and 
 year , till that Queen of England and her bridegroom have become part 
 and parcel of the tale of the " Three Golden Oranges , " of the " King of 
 Portugal 's cowherd , " of the " wonderful little Blue Bird , " and such - like 
 story in the mind of the child of those apennine city . the Queen 
 of England go to meet her bridegroom at the Holy House of Loreto . the 
 notion , even to we , do savour strangely of the fairy tale . 

 what be , meanwhile , the thought of the beautiful little fairy 
 princess , with laugh dark eye and shin golden hair , and brilliant 
 fair skin , more brilliant for the mysterious patch of rouge upon 
 the cheek , and vermilion upon the lip , whom the more audacious or 
 fortunate of the townsfolk catch a glimpse of seat in her gorgeous 
 travel dress   for the eighteenth century be still in its stage of 
 pre - revolutionary brocade and gold lace and powder and spangle   behind 
 the curtain of the coach ? Louise , Princess of Stolberg - Gedern , and 
 ex - canoness of Mons , be , if we may judge by the crayon portrait and the 
 miniature do about that time , much more of a child than most woman of 
 nineteen . a clever and accomplished young lady , but , one would say , 
 with , as yet , more intelligence and acquire pretty little habit and 
 idea than character ; a childish woman of the world , a bright , light 
 handful of thistle - bloom . and thus , besides the confusion , the unreality 
 due to precipitation of event and change of scene , the sense that she 
 have   how long ago - day , week , or year ? in such a state time become a 
 great muddle and mystery   be actually marry by proxy , that she have 
 come the whole way from Paris , through Venice and across the sea , 
 besides be in this dream - like , phantasmagoric condition , which must 
 have make all thing seem light - it be probable that the young lady have 
 scarcely sufficient consciousness of herself as a grown - up , independent , 
 independently feel and think creature , to feel or think very 
 strongly over her situation . it be the regular thing for girl of 
 Louise of Stolberg 's rank to be put through a certain amount of rather 
 vague convent education , as she have be at mon ; to be put through a 
 certain amount of ball and party ; to be put through the formality of 
 betrothal and marriage ; all this be the half - conscious dream - then 
 would come the great wake up . and Louise of Stolberg be , most likely , 
 in a state of feeling like that which come to we with the early 
 light through the blind : pleasant , or unpleasant ? we know not which ; 
 still drowse , dream , but yet strongly conscious that in a moment we 
 shall be awake to reality . 

 there be , nevertheless , in the position of this girl something which , 
 even in these circumstance , must have compel she to think , or , at 
 all event , to meditate , however confusedly , upon the present and the 
 future . if she have in she the small spark of imagination she must 
 have feel , to an acute degree , the sort of continuous surprise , recur 
 like the tick of a clock , which haunt we sometimes with the fact that 
 it really do just happen to be ourselves to whom some curious lot , 
 some rare combination of the number in life 's lottery , have come . for 
 the man whom she be go to marry - nay , to whom , in a sense , she be 
 married already - the unknown whom she would see for the first time that 
 evening , be not the mere typical bridegroom , the mere man of rank and 
 fortune , to whom , whatever his particular individual shape and name , the 
 daughter of a high - bear but impoverished house have know herself , since 
 her childhood , to be devote . 

 Louise Maximilienne Caroline Emanuele , daughter of the late Prince 
 Gustavus Adolphus of Stolberg - Gedern , Prince of the Empire , who have 
 die , a Colonel of Maria Theresa , in the battle of Leuthen ; and of 
 Elisabeth Philippine , Countess of Horn , bear at mon in Hainaut , the 
 20th September 1752 , educate there in a convent , and subsequently 
 admit to the half - ecclesiastic , half - worldly dignity of Canoness of 
 Ste . Wandru in that town : Louise , Princess of Stolberg , now in her 
 twentieth year , have be betroth , and , a few week ago , marry by 
 proxy in Paris to Charles Edward Stuart , know to history as the Younger 
 Pretender , to popular imagination as Bonnie Prince Charlie , and to 
 society in the second half of the eighteenth century as the Count of 
 Albany . the match have be make up hurriedly - most probably without 
 consulting , or dream of consulting , the girl - by her mother , the 
 dowager Princess Stolberg , and the Duke of Fitz - James , Charles Edward 's 
 cousin . the french Minister , Duc d'Aiguillon , in one of those fit of 
 prepare Charles Edward as a weapon against England , which have more 
 than once cost the Pretender so much bitterness , and the Court of 
 Versailles so much brazenly endure shame , have intimate to the Count of 
 Albany that he have well take unto himself a wife . Charles Edward have 
 more than once refuse ; this time he accept , and his cousin Fitz - James 
 look around for a possible future Queen of England . now it happen 
 that the eld son of Fitz - James , the Marquis of Jamaica and Duke of 
 Berwick , have just marry Caroline , the second daughter of the widow of 
 Prince Gustavus Adolphus of Stolberg - Gedern ; so that the choice 
 naturally fall upon this lady 's eld sister , Louise of Stolberg , the 
 young canoness of Ste . Wandru of Mons . 

 the alliance , short of royal birth , be , in the matter of dignity , all 
 that could be wish ; the Stolbergs be one of the most illustrious 
 family of the Holy Roman Empire , in whose service they have discharge 
 many high office ; the Horns , on the other hand , be among the most 
 brilliant of the flemish aristocracy , ally to the Gonzagas of Mantua , 
 the Colonna , Orsinis , the Medina Celis , Croys , Lignes , Hohenzollerns , 
 and the house of Lorraine , reign or quasi - reigning family ; and 
 Louise of Stolberg 's mother be , moreover , on the maternal side , the 
 grand - daughter of the Earl of Elgin and Ailesbury , a Bruce , and a 
 staunch follower of King James II . such have be the inducement in the 
 eye of the Duke of Fitz - James ; and therefore in the eye of Charles 
 Edward , for whom he be commission to select a wife . the inducement 
 to the Princess of Stolberg have be even great . foremost among they 
 be probably the mere desire of rid herself , poor and live as she 
 be on the charity of the Empress - Queen , of another of the four girl 
 with whom she have be leave a widow at twenty - five . it have be a great 
 blessing to get the two eld girl , Louise and Caroline , educate , 
 house for a time , and momentarily settle in the world by their 
 admission to the rich and noble chapter of Ste . Wandru : it must have 
 be a great blessing to see the second girl marry to the son of 
 Fitz - James ; it would be a still great one to get Louise safely off her 
 hand , now that the third and fourth daughter require to be think 
 of . so far for the desirability of any marriage . this particular 
 marriage with Prince Charles Edward be , moreover , such as to tempt the 
 vanity and ambition of a lady like the widow Princess of Stolberg , 
 conscious of her high rank , and conscious , perhaps painfully conscious 
 of the difficulty of live up to its requirement . the Count of 
 Albany 's grandfather have be King of England ; his father , the Pretender 
 James , have live with royal state in his exile at Rome , recognise as 
 reign Sovereign by the Pope , and even , every now and then , by France 
 and Spain . no Government have recognise Charles Edward as King of 
 England ; but , on the other hand , Charles Edward have virtually be king 
 of Scotland during the ' 45 ; he have be promise the help of France to 
 restore he to his right ; and although that help have never be 
 satisfactorily give in the past , who could tell whether it might 
 not be give at any moment in the future ? the up and down of politic 
 bring all sort of unexpected necessity ; and why should the french 
 Government , which have ignominiously kidnap and bundle off Charles 
 Edward in 1748 , have send for he again only a year ago , have urge he 
 to marry , unless it have some scheme for reinstate he in England ? the 
 Duke of Fitz - James have doubtless urge these consideration ; he have not 
 lay much weight on the fact that Charles Edward be thirty - two year 
 old than his propose wife ; still less be it probable that he have bid 
 the Princess of Stolberg consider that his royal kinsman be say to be 
 neither of very good health , nor of very agreeable disposition , nor of 
 very temperate habit ; or , if such idea be present to the Princess 
 Stolberg , she put they behind she . be it as it may , these be matter 
 for the judicious consideration of a mother ; not , certainly , for the 
 thought of a daughter . the judicious mother decide that such a match 
 be a good one ; perhaps , in her heart , she be even overwhelm by the 
 glory which this daughter of her be permit by Heaven to add to all 
 the glory of the illustrious Stolbergs and Horns . anyhow , she accept 
 eagerly ; so eagerly as to forget both gratitude and prudence : for so far 
 from consult her benefactress , Maria Theresa , about the advisability 
 of this marriage , or ask her sovereign permission for a step 
 which might draw upon the Empress - Queen some disagreeable diplomatic 
 correspondence with England , the Princess of Stolberg keep the matter 
 close , and do not even announce the marriage to the Court of Vienna ; 
 yet she must have foresee what occur , namely , that Maria Theresa , 
 mortify not merely in her dignity as a sovereign , but also , and 
 perhaps more , in her rule passion of benevolent meddlesomeness , would 
 suspend the pension which form a large portion of the Princess 's 
 income , and compel she to the abject apology before restore it . the 
 marriage with Charles Edward Stuart be worth all that ! 

 Louise of Stolberg be probably well aware of the extreme glory of the 
 marriage for which she have be reserve . the Fitz - Jameses , in virtue of 
 their illegitimate descent from James II . , consider themselves and 
 be consider as a sort of prince of the blood ; and as such they 
 doubtless impress Louise with a great notion of the glory of the 
 Stuarts , and the absolute legitimacy of their claim . on his marriage 
 Charles Edward assume the title , and attempt to assume the position , 
 of King of England ; so his bride must have consider herself as the 
 wife not merely of the Count of Albany , but of Charles III . , King of 
 Great Britain , France , and Ireland . she be go to be a queen ! we 
 must try , we democratic creature of a time when king and queen may 
 perfectly be adventurer and adventuress , to put ourselves in the 
 place of this young lady of a century ago , bring up as a dignitary 
 of a chapter into which admission depend entirely upon the number 
 and quality of quartering of the candidate 's escutcheon , under a 
 superior - the Abbess of Ste . Wandru - who be the sister of the late 
 Emperor Francis , the sister - in - law of Maria Theresa ; we must try and 
 conceive an institution something between a school , a sisterhood , and a 
 club , in which the rule idea , the source of all dignity , jealousy , 
 envy , and triumph , be greatness of birth and connection ; we must try 
 and do this in order to understand what , to Louise of Stolberg , be the 
 full value of the fact of become the wife of Charles Edward Stuart . 
 one hundred and twelve year ago , and seventeen year before the great 
 revolution which yawn , an almost impassable gulf , between we and the 
 man and woman of the past , a woman , a girl of nineteen , and a canoness 
 of Ste . Wandru of Mons , need have be of no base temper if , on the 
 eve of such a wedding as this one , her mind have be full of only one 
 idea : the idea , monotonous and drowningly loud like some big cathedral 
 bell , " I shall be a queen . " but if Louise of Stolberg be , as be most 
 probable , in some such a state of vague exultation , we must remember 
 also that there may well have enter into such exultation an element 
 with which even we , and even the most austerely or snobbishly democratic 
 among we , might fully have sympathise . her mother , her sister , her 
 brother - in - law , and the old Duke of Fitz - James , who have make up her 
 marriage and marry she by proxy , and every other person who have 
 approach she during the last month , must have be fill the mind of 
 Louise of Stolberg with tale of the ' 45 and of the heroism of Prince 
 Charlie . and her mind , which , as afterwards appear , be romantic , 
 fascinate by eccentricity and genius , may easily have become enamour 
 of the bridegroom who await she , the last of so brilliant and ill - fat 
 a race , the hero of Gladsmuir and Falkirk , at whose approach the 
 Londoners have shut their shop in terror , and the hanoverian usurper 
 order his yacht to lie ready moor at the Tower step ; the more than 
 royal young man whom   as the Jacobites doubtless tell she   only the 
 foolish and traitorous obstinacy of his follower have prevent from 
 reinstate his father on the throne of England . historical figure , 
 especially those of a heroic sort , remain picture in man 's mind at 
 their moment of glory ; and this be the case particularly with the Young 
 Pretender , who have disappear into well - nigh complete mystery after his 
 wonderful exploit and hairbreadth escape of the ' 45 ; so that in the 
 eye of Louise of Stolberg the man she be about to marry appear most 
 probably but little change from the brilliant youth who have march on 
 foot at the head of his army towards London , who have hold court at 
 Holyrood and roam in disguise about the Hebrides . 

 still , it be difficult to imagine that as the hour of meeting draw 
 nearer , the little Princess , as her travel carriage toil up the 
 Apennine valley , do not feel some terror of the future and the 
 unknown . the spring come late to those region ; in the middle of April 
 the blackthorn be scarcely bud on the rock , the violet be still 
 plentiful underneath the leafless roadside hedge ; scarcely a faint 
 yellow , more like autumn that spring , be begin to tinge the scraggy 
 outline of the poplar , which rise in spectral regiment out of the 
 river bed . wherever the valley widen , or the road gain some hill - crest , 
 a huge peak white with newly - fall snow confront you , close in the 
 view , bring bleakness and bitterness curiously home to the feeling . 
 these valley , torrent - track between the steep rock of livid basalt or 
 bright red sandstone , bare as a bone or thinly clothe with ilex and 
 juniper scrub , be inexpressibly lonely and sad , especially at this time 
 of year . you feel imprison among the rock in a sort of catacomb open 
 to the sky , where the shadow gather in the early afternoon , and only 
 the light on the snow - peak and on the high - sailing cloud tell you 
 that the sun be still in the heaven . village there seem none ; and you 
 may drive for an hour without meet more than a stray peasant cutting 
 scrub or quarry gravel on the hill - side , a train of mule carry 
 charcoal or faggot ; the town be far between , bleak , black , filthy , 
 and such as only to make you feel all the more poignantly the utter 
 desolateness of these mountain . no sad way of enter Italy can 
 well be imagine than land at Ancona and cross through the 
 Apennines to Rome in the early spring . to a girl accustom to the fat 
 flatness of Flanders , to the market - bustle of a Flemish provincial town , 
 this journey must have be overwhelmingly dreary and dismal . during 
 those long hour drag up these Apennine valley , do a shadow fall 
 across the mind of the pretty , fair - haired , brilliant - complexion 
 little canoness of Mons , a shadow like the cold melancholy blue which 
 fill the valley between the sun - smite peak ? and do it ever occur 
 to she , as the horse be change in the little post - town , that it be 
 in honour of Holy Week that the savage - looking bearded man , the big , 
 brawny , madonna - like woman have get on their good clothe ? do it strike 
 she that the unplastered church - front be drape with black , the 
 street strew with laurel and box , as for a funeral , that the bell 
 be silent in their tower ? perhaps not ; and yet when , a few year 
 later , the Countess of Albany be already will not to say that her married 
 life have be just such as befit a woman who have go to the altar on 
 Good Friday , she must have remember , and the remembrance must have 
 seem fraught with ill oman , that last day of her girlhood , travel 
 through the black deserted valley of the March , through the 
 world - forget mountain - town with their hush bell and black - drape 
 church and funereally strew street . 

 at Loreto - where , as a good Catholic , the Princess Louise of Stolberg 
 doubtless pray for a blessing on her marriage , in the great sanctuary 
 which enclose with silver and carve marble the little house of the 
 Virgin - at Loreto the bride be meet by a Jacobite dignitary , Lord 
 Carlyle , and five servant in the crimson livery of England . at 
 Macerata , one of the large town of the March of Ancona , she be 
 await by her bridegroom . a noble family of the province , the 
 Compagnoni - Marefoschis , one of whom , a cardinal , be an old friend of 
 the Stuarts , have place their palace at the disposal of the royal pair . 
 we most of we know what such palace , in small italian provincial town 
 south of the Apennines , be apt to be ; huge , gloomy , shapeless mass of 
 brickwork and mouldering plaster , something between a mediæval fortress 
 and a convent ; great black archway , where the refuse of the house , the 
 filth of the town , have peaceably accumulate   and how much more in those 
 day ; magnificent statue staircase give over to the few servant 
 who have replace the armed bravos of two century ago ; long suite 
 of room , vast , resound like so many church , glaze in the last 
 century with tiny square of bad glass , through which the light 
 come green and thick as through sea - water ; carpet still despise 
 as a new - fangled luxury from France ; the wall , not cheerful with 
 eighteenth - century french panel and hanging , but cover with big naked 
 frescoed man and woman , or fade arra ; few fire - place , but those few 
 enormous , look like a huge red cavern in the room . the Marefoschis

 fill with torch and wax light ; a funereal illumination in a 
 funereal place , it must have seem to the little Princess of Stolberg , 
 fresh from the brilliant nattiness of the parisian house of the time of 
 Louis XV . 

 the bride alight ; a small , plump , well - proportion , rather childish 
 creature , with still half - form childish feature , a trifle snub , a 
 trifle soulless , very pretty , tender , light - hearted ; a charming little 
 creature , very well make to steal folk 's heart unconscious to 
 themselves and to herself . 

 the bridegroom meet she . a fade , but extremely characteristic crayon 
 portrait , the companion of the one of which I have already speak , now

 take at this time , have show I what that bridegroom must have be . 
 the man who meet Louise of Stolberg at Macerata as her husband and

 dress usually , and doubtless on this occasion , with the blue ribbon

 CHAPTER II . 

 the BRIDEGROOM . 


 on the Wednesday after Easter the bride and bridegroom make their solemn 
 entry into Rome ; the two travel carriage of the Prince and of the 
 Princess be draw by six horse ; four gala coach , carry the 
 attendant of Charles Edward and of his brother the Cardinal Duke of 
 York , follow behind , and the street be clear by four outrider 
 dress in scarlet with the white Stuart cockade . the house to which 
 Louise of Stolberg , now Louise d'Albany , or rather , as she sign 
 herself at this time , Louise R. , be conduct after her five day ' 
 wedding journey , have pass through several hand since belong to the 
 Sacchettis , the Muti Papazzurris , and now - a - day to the family of 
 About 's charming and unhappy Tolla Ferraldi . clement XI . have give or 
 lend it to the Elder Pretender : James III . , as he be style in Italy , 
 have settle in it about 1719 with his beautiful bride Maria Clementina 
 Sobieska , romantically filch by her Jacobites from the convent at 
 Innsbruck , where the Emperor Charles VI . have hop to restrain she from 
 so compromise a match ; here , in the year 1720 , Charles Edward have be 
 bear and have his baby finger kiss by the whole sacred college ; and 
 here the so - call King of England have die at last , a melancholy 
 hypochondriac , in 1766 . the palace close in the narrow end of the 
 square of the Santissimi Apostoli , stately and quiet with its various 
 palace , Colonna , Odescalchi , and whatever else their name , and its 
 pillare church front . there be a certain aristocratic serenity about 
 that square , separate , like a big palace yard , from the bustling Corso 
 in front ; yet to I there remain , a tradition of my childhood , a sort 
 of grotesque and horrid suggestiveness connect with this peaceful and 
 princely corner of Rome . for , many year ago , when the square of the 
 Santissimi Apostoli be still periodically strew with sand that the 
 Pope might not be jolt when his golden coach drive up to the church , 
 and when the name of Charles Edward and his Countess be curiously 
 mix up in my brain with those of Charles the First and Mary Queen of 
 Scots , there use to be in a little street lead out of the square 
 towards the Colonna Gardens , a dark recess in the blank church - wall , an 
 embrasure , shelter by a pen - house roof and raise like a stage a few 
 steep step above the pavement ; and in it loom , strap to a chair , 
 dark in the shadow , a creature in a long black robe and a skull cap 
 draw close over his head ; a vague , contort , writhe and gibber 
 horror , of whose St. Vitus twisting and mouthing we child scarcely 
 venture to catch a glimpse as we hurry up the narrow street , follow 
 by the bestial cry and moan of the solitary maniac . this weird and 
 grotesque sight , more weird and more grotesque see through a muddled 
 childish fancy and through the haze of year , have remain associate in 
 my mind with that particular corner of Rome , where , with window look 
 down upon that street , upon that blank church - wall with its little 
 black recess , the palace of the Stuarts close in the narrow end of the 
 square of the Santissimi Apostoli . and now , I can not help see a 
 certain strange appropriateness in the fact that the image of that 
 mouth and gesticulate half - witte creature should be connect in 
 my mind with the house to which , with pomp of six - horse coach and 
 scarlet outrider , Charles Edward Stuart conduct his bride .

  from a pastel , painter unknown , once in the possession of the

 for the beautiful and brilliant youth who have secretly leave that palace 
 twenty - four year before to re - conquer his father 's kingdom , the gentle 
 and gallant and chivalric young prince of whose irresistible manner and 
 voice the canny chieftain have vainly bid each other beware when he 
 land with his handful of friend and call the Highlanders to arm ; 
 the patient and heroic exile , sing to his friend when the sea wash 
 over their boat and the hanoverian soldier surround their cavern or 
 hovel , who have silently give Miss Macdonald that solemn kiss which she 
 treasure for more than fifty year in her strong heart - that Charles 
 Edward Stuart be now a creature not much worthy and not much less 
 repulsive than the poor idiot whom I still see , fling about his 
 palsy hand and gobble with his speechless mouth , beneath the 
 window of the Stuart palace . the taste for drinking , so strange in a 
 man bring up to the age of twenty - three among the proverbially sober 
 Italians , have arise in Charles Edward , a most excusable ill habit in 
 one continually expose to wet and cold , frequently sleep on the damp 
 ground , ill - feed , anxious , wear out by over - exertion in fly before his 
 enemy , during those frightful month after the defeat at Culloden , 
 when , with a price of thirty thousand pound upon his head , he have 
 lurk in the fastness of the Hebrides . we hear that on the eve of his 
 final escape from Scotland , his host , Macdonald of Kingsburgh , prevent

 the HEIR .

 CHAPTER VI . 

 ALFIERI . 


 the childhood and early youth of Vittorio Alfieri have be strangely 
 vacant , dreary , one might almost say intellectually and morally sordid ; 
 and the strange , the dreary circumstance about they be exactly 
 that this vacuity , this dreariness , this total want of all that can make

 the word numbness return to my mind in connexion with this young

 the one incident which shed light upon the lad 's morbid constitution

 CHAPTER VII . 

 the CAVALIERE servente . 


 Alfieri 's great terror in life be to fall in love once more . all his 
 love affair have be degrade to his good sense , his will and his 
 manhood ; they have be odious , even at the moment , to his extraordinary 
 innate passion , or , one might almost say , monomania for independence ; 
 he who even in his dull and most inane year have hat the thought 
 of any sort of military or diplomatic position which should imply 
 subjection to a despotic government , whose only strong feeling about the 
 world in general have long be a fierce hatred and contempt both for 
 those who tyrannise and those who be tyrannise over , this Alfieri 
 have always , as he tell we , flee , though unsuccessfully , from the 
 presence of woman whose social position   though the word sound like a 
 sarcasm   be sufficiently good to make any regular love intrigue 
 possible or probable . how much more must he not defend his liberty now 
 that he see before he the direct road to glory , and feel within himself 
 the power to journey along it . 

 thus it be , as he explain in his autobiography , that on his first 
 arrival in Florence , hear everyone praise the character and talent 
 of the wife of Charles Edward Stuart , and see the beautiful young 
 woman at theatre and in the public promenade , he resolutely decline to

  I be translate his word , and must explain that her eye , which 
 seem blue to bonstetten and dark to Alfieri 's , be in reality of that 
 hazel colour which give great prominence to the pupil , and therefore 
 leave the idea of black eye   contrast with the brilliant fair skin 
 and pale blonde hair , of the graciousness and sweetness and perhaps even 
 a certain sad austerity in her whole appearance and manner , - all this

 but after some month at Siena , where his thought have be entirely

 sienese professor , after that first feeling of attraction have die

 a different individual from that inane , dull , violent young man who in 
 the vacuity of life have rag and roar in the chain of unworthy love . 
 and she , she also , be quite a different woman from the Lady Ligonier 
 and from the Marchesa di Prié , the shameless , unfaithful wife , and 
 heartless , vain , worldly coquette who have make such havoc of his heart . 
 she be a cold , virtuous , extremely intellectual woman , try to

 study , in abstract interest which should take away her thought from

 friend to a poet , and who would know how to value his friendship . and 
 he , continually seek for people who could understand his literary

 something very reassuring to Alfieri 's apprehension in the knowledge

 cicisbeism , but with a foreigner , the jealously - guard wife of a sort 
 of legendary ogre , with whom , however much the old fury of love might 
 awaken in he , there could by no possibility be anything beyond the most 
 strictly watch friendship . so Alfieri go to the palace of the Count 
 of Albany ; and , have once be , return there . 

 the palace buy by Charles Edward about 1776 stand in the most remote 
 and peaceful quarter of Florence . a few quiet street , unbroken by 
 shop - front and unfrequented by vehicle , lead up to that quarter ; 
 street of low white - wash convent wall overtop by tree , of silent 
 palace , of unpretende little house of the seventeenth or eighteenth 
 century , from behind whose iron window - grating and blister green 
 shutter one expect even now , as one pass in the silence of the 
 summer afternoon , to hear the faint jangle of some harpsichord - strum 
 minuet , the turn and sudden high note of some long - forget song by 
 Cimarosa or Paisiello . it be a region of dead wall , over which bend 
 the acacia and elm , over which shoot up the cypress and cedar 
 of innumerable convent and palace - garden , on whose flower - bed and 
 fountain and quincunxe the first - floor window look down . in the midst 
 of all this , at the corner of two very quiet street , stand the palace , 
 now of the Duke of San Clemente , an ungainly , yellow structure of 
 various epoch , with a pretty late sixteenth - century belvedere tower on 
 one side ; a lot of shuttered and heavily - grate seventeenth - century 
 window , ornament with stone stay - lace and tag , upon the dark 
 street ; and to the back a desolate old garden , where the vine have 
 crawl over the stonework , and the grotesque seventeenth - century

 ill - trim hedge of ilex and laurel : the most old - world house and 
 garden in the old - world part of the town . the eighteenth century still 
 seem very near as we walk in those street and look in , through the 
 railing , at the ilex and laurel quincunxe , the lichene statue of 
 that garden ; and from the roof of the house still float , creak in 
 the wind , regardless of the triumph of the Hanoverians , unconscious of 
 the many banner which have be throw , mere heap of obsolete colour 
 tatter , on the dust - heap , a rusty metal weather - vane , bear the

 raise in Glenfinnan . 

 in this house be now develop one of the most singular love that 
 ever be . shortly after his introduction to the Countess of Albany , 
 Alfieri , terrify lest he might be forfeit his spiritual liberty

 the grave and virtuous Gori answer that he should not : this new love 
 have be send to he as a cure for all base love ; instead of crush 
 it as an obstacle to his high life and his glory , he should thankfully

 let we pause , and consider for a moment the meaning of Alfieri 's 
 question , and the meaning of Gori 's answer ; let we try and realise the 
 idea and feeling of two honourable man , seek a high life , in a 
 country so near our own as Italy , and so short a while ago as the year 
 1777 . here be Alfieri , passionately desirous to redeem his own 
 existence by intellectual effort , and confident of a vague mission to 
 awaken his countryman to his own nobler feeling : to the contempt of 
 sensual pleasure and worldly vanity , the hatred of political and 
 religious servitude , the love of truth and justice , the love of Italy . 
 here be this Alfieri , at the very outset of his new career , solemnly 
 confide to his kind and wise friend the scruple , the fear , 
 which restrain he from seek the company of a woman whom he be 
 begin to love , and who be begin to love he , a young woman 
 marry by mere worldly convention to a sickly , brutal , and brutish 
 drunkard , old enough to be her father . and what be these scruple ? 
 merely that a new love might distract Alfieri from his plan of study 
 and work , that a woman might cheat he of glory , and Italy of the tragic 
 drama which would school she to virtue . that there could be any other 
 scruple appear never to have cross Alfieri 's brain : that there could 
 be any reason to pause and ask himself whether he be do wrong or ill 
 before expose to temptation the woman whom he love , and the honour 
 which he love more than she ; whether he have a right to return to the 
 palace of Charles Edward and , while receive his hospitality , while

 but to the Italian of the eighteenth century , even to the man who most

 I scarcely know whether it be a thing about which to be cheerful or sad ,

 the ESCAPE .

 CHAPTER IX . 

 ROME . 


 this strange intellectual passion , the meeting , as it be , of two

  2 in the first day of December 1780 a certain Mme . Orlandini , a

 there must always be something which shock we in the behaviour ,

 work out their happiness in despite of the evil world in which they 
 live , the Countess and Alfieri , infinitely intellectually and morally 
 superior to many of we whom circumstance permit to live blameless and 
 comfortable , be splash with the mud of unrighteousness , which be 
 foreign to their nature , and remain spot in the eye of posterity . 

 Charles Edward do what he have do once before in his life : he apply 
 to the Government to put he again in possession of the woman whom he 
 have victimise ; but as the French Government have refuse to recognise 
 his claim over his fugitive mistress , so the Government of the Grand 
 Duke of Tuscany now refuse to give he back his fugitive wife . the 
 Countess of Albany have naturally take no clothe with she in her 
 flight ; and she presently send a maid to the palace in Via San Sebastiano 
 to fetch such thing as she might require . but Charles Edward would not 
 permit a single one of her effect to be touch ; if she want she 
 clothe and trinket , she might come and fetch they herself . however , 
 after a few day , a message come from the Pope , order the pretender 
 to supply his wife with whatever she might require ; a threat to suspend 
 the pension be probably express or imply , for Charles Edward 
 immediately obey . 

 meanwhile , the Countess of Albany be anxiously await at the convent 
 of the Bianchette a decision from her brother - in - law , to whom she have 
 write immediately after her flight . those first day must have be 
 painfully unquiet . what if the Tuscan Court should listen to the Count 
 of Albany 's entreaty ? what if Cardinal York should take part with his 
 brother ? return to the house of her husband would be death or bad 
 than death . Cardinal York answer immediately : a long , kind , rather 
 weak - minded letter , the ideal letter of a well - intentione , rather 
 silly priest , in curious anglo - roman French . he inform she that for

 convent in Rome , whither he invite she to remove as soon as possible . 
 in January 1781 the Countess of Albany , accompany by a Mme . de Marzan , 
 who appear to have form part of her household , and two maid , start

 raving to get his wife back , that Alfieri and Gahagan , armed and 
 dress as servant , accompany the carriage a considerable part of its

 anyone who would kill Alfieri ; and even in that humdrum late eighteenth

 waylay a carriage and kidnap a woman . 

 the Countess of Albany be instal in the Ursuline convent in Via 
 Vittoria , a street near the Piazza di Spagna . a gloomy family memory

 she have leave Florence at the end of the year ; in the spring she see 
 Alfieri again . the quiet work which have seem so natural and easy while

 he hurry to the Ursuline convent , and be admit to speak to the 
 Countess of Albany . " I see she , " he write many year later , " but   o God ! 
 my heart seem to break at the mere recollection   I see she a prisoner

 CHAPTER X. 

 antigone . 


 about three month after the Countess of Albany 's flight from her 
 husband , the Pope grant her permission to leave the Ursuline convent ;

 magnificent palace of the Cancelleria . Alfieri be at Naples when he 
 receive this news , rid gloomily along the sea - shore , weeping

 eighteenth century , there be no incongruity in a would - be ancient roman 
 shed love - sick tear , unable to give his attention to work , live , 
 as he express it , on the come in and go out of the post . " I 
 wish to return to Rome , " he write , " and at the same time I feel very 
 keenly that I ought not to do it yet . the struggle between love and 
 duty which take place in an honourable and tender heart , be the most 
 terrible and mortal pain that a man can suffer . I delay throughout 
 April , and I determine to drag on through May ; but on the 12th May I 
 find myself , I scarcely know how , back in Rome . " 

 Alfieri find the Countess of Albany establish in the palace of the 
 Cancelleria , the mistress of the establishment , for her brother - in - law

 roman society , which be even a shade bad than those of Florence ,

 passive , easily mould by a more vehement nature , love Alfieri much

 deliberately make up , the artificial , have a charm for she . be this

 Florence . 

 and these month in Rome seem to have be the happy month of 
 Alfieri 's life , the happy , probably , of the life of the Countess 
 of Albany . Alfieri hire the villa Strozzi , on the Esquiline , a small

 spend lavishly on book , and more lavishly on horse , on horse which , 
 accord to his own account , be his third passion , come only after 
 his love for Mme . d'Albany , and sometimes usurp the place of his love 
 of literary glory . 

 the mania for systematic division of his time , the invincible tendency 
 to routine , which follow in most Italians after the disorder and 
 wastefulness of youth , have already get the well of Alfieri . he have , 
 almost at the moment when the passion for literature first disclose 
 itself , make up his mind to write a definite number of tragedy , first 
 twelve , then fourteen , and no more ; and to devote a certain number of 
 year to the elaborate process of first construct they mentally , then 
 of write they full length in prose , and finally of turn this prose 
 into verse ; and he be later to devise a corresponding plan of write 
 an equally fix number of comedy and satire in an equally fix 
 number of year , after which , as we have see , he be to give up his 
 thought , have attain the age of forty - five , to prepare for death . 

 this routine be a national characteristic , and absorb many an italian , 
 turn all the poetry of his nature to prose , with a kind of dreadful 
 inevitableness ; but Alfieri do not merely submit to routine , he enjoy 
 it , he devise and carry it out with all the ferocity of his nature . 
 to this man , who care so much for the figure he cut , and so little for 
 all the thing which surround he , a life reduce to absolute monotony 
 of grind work be almost an object of æsthetic pleasure , almost 
 an object of sensual delight : he enjoy a dead level , an endless 
 white - wash wall , as much as other man , and especially other poet , 
 enjoy the up and down , the irregularity and mottled colour of 
 existence . so Alfieri arrange for himself , in his house near Santa 
 Maria Maggiore , what to he be a life of exquisite delightfulness . 

 he spend the whole early morning read the latin and italian classic , 
 and grind away at his tragedy , which , after repeat sketch out , 
 repeat write out in prose , be now go through the most elaborate 
 process of writing , re - writing , revising , and re - revise in verse . 
 then , before resume his solitary study in the afternoon , he would 
 have one of his many horse saddle , and ride about in the desolate 
 tract of the town , which in papal time extend from Santa Maria 
 Maggiore to the Porta Pia , the Porta San Lorenzo , and St. John Lateran : 
 mile of former villa gardens , with quincunxe and flower - bed , cut up 
 for cabbage - grow , wide open space where the wall of a temple , the 
 arch of an aqueduct , rise crown with wall - flower and weed out of the 
 rank grass , the briar and nettle , the heap of broken masonry and 
 plaster , among which shine beneath the dart lizard , scrap of 
 vermilion wall - fresco , the chip of purple porphyry or dark - green 
 serpentine ; long avenue of tree early sere , close in by arum - fringe 
 wall , or by ditch where the wither reed creak beneath the 
 festoon of clematis and wild vine ; solemn and solitary wilderness 
 within the city wall , where the silence be break only by the lowing

 present , a border - land of the past , and which , as Alfieri say , think

 and wistfully look out on to the green desolation islande with ruin 
 and traverse by the vast procession of the aqueduct , invite one to 
 meditate , and cry , and be a poet . and sometimes - we know it from the

 lust which have turn these spot into a wilderness , and which have leave 
 the world , as Alfieri always feel , and a man not unlike Alfieri in 
 savage and destructive austerity , St. Just , be later to say , empty 
 since the day of the Romans . 

 towards dusk Alfieri put by his book , and descend through the twilit 
 street of the upper city - where the troop of red and yellow and blue 
 seminarist , and black and brown monk , pass by like ant , homeward

 burn two century before by Cardinal York 's predecessor in that big

 blonde woman with the dark hazel eye , whom Alfieri speak of as his

 merely " the poet , " so completely have these strange , self - model , 
 unconsciously - attitudinise lover , array themselves and their love 
 accord to the pattern of Dante and Petrarch . 

 to the Countess , we may be sure , Alfieri never fail to give a most 
 elaborate account of his day 's work , nor to read to she whatever scene 
 of his play he have block out , in prose , or work up in verse . by 11 
 o'clock , he tell we , he be always back in his solitary little villa on 
 the Esquiline . 

 but this , although it be probably correct with regard to his visit to 
 Mme . d'Albany , with whom consideration for gossip prevent his staying 
 much after ten at night , must not be take as the invariable rule ; for 
 Alfieri , devote as he be to his lady , by no mean neglect other 
 society . he be finish his allotted number of tragedy , and , as the 
 solemn moment of publication approach , he begin to be torment with 
 that same desire to display his work to other , to hear their praise 
 even if false , to understand their opinion even if unfavourable , which 
 come , by gust , as one of the passion of his life . Rome be at that 
 time , like every italian town , full of literary academy , conventicle 
 of very small intellectual fry meeting in private drawing - room or at 
 coffee - house , and sway by the overlordship of the famous Arcadia , 
 which have now sink into be a huge club to which every creature who 
 scribble , or daub , or strum , or have a coach - and - pair , or a bad 
 tongue , or a pretty face , or a title , belong without further claim . 
 there be also several house of woman who affect intelligence or 
 culture , have no claim to beauty or fashion ; and foremost among these , 
 but differ from they by the real originality and culture of the lady 
 of the house , the charm of her young daughter , and the superior quality 
 of the conversation and music to be enjoy there , be the house of a 
 Signora Maria Pizzelli , of all woman in Rome the one to whom , after the 
 Countess of Albany , Alfieri show himself most assiduous . in her house 
 and in many other Alfieri begin to give almost public reading of his 
 play ; try to persuade himself that his object in so do be to 
 judge , from the expression of face and even more from the restlessness 
 or quiescence of his listener on their chair , how his work might 
 affect the mixed audience of a theatre ; but admit in his heart of 
 heart that the old desire to be remark have as much to do with these 
 exhibition as with the six - horse gallop which use to astonish the 
 people of Turin and Florence . 

 but something well soon offer itself . the Duke Grimaldi have have a 
 small theatre construct in the spanish palace , his residence as 
 Ambassador from the Catholic King , and a small company of high - bear 
 amateur have be play in it translation of french comedy and 
 tragedy . to these lady and gentleman Alfieri offer his Antigone , 
 which be accept with fervour . the beautiful and majestic Duchess 
 of Zagarolo be to act the part of the heroine ; her brother and 
 sister - in - law , the Duke and Duchess of Ceri , respectively the part of 
 Hæmon and of Argia , while the character of Creon , the villain of the 
 piece , be reserve for Alfieri himself . the performance of Antigone 
 be a great solemnity . the magnificent room of the Spanish Embassy be 
 crowd with the fashionable world of Rome , which , in the year 1782 , 
 include priest and prince of the Church quite as much as paint 
 lady and powdered cavalier . a contemporary diary , keep by the page of 
 the Princess Colonna , a certain Abate Benedetti , enable we to form some

 beauty , equally famous for their conquest in the ecclesiastical as 
 well as the secular nobility , the Princess Santacroce and the Princess 
 Altieri , vie with each other in the magnificence of their diamond and 
 of their lace , and each upon the arm of a prince of the Church who have 
 the honour of be her orthodox cavaliere servente ; the Princess 
 Altieri lead in by Cardinal Giovan Francesco Albani , the very gallant and 
 art - love nephew of Winckelmann 's Cardinal Alessandro ; the Princess 
 Santacroce escort by the french Ambassador Cardinal de Bernis , the 
 amiable society rhymester of Mme . de Pompadour , whom Frederick the Great 
 have surname Babet la bouquetière . in the front row sit the wife of 
 the Senator Rezzonico , who , in virtue of be the niece of the late 
 Pope Clement XIII . , affect an almost royal pomp , and by her side sit 
 the witty and most literary of the Sacred College , the still very 
 flirtatious old Cardinal Gerdil . the hall be nearly full when the stir 
 in the crowd , and the general look in one direction , announce the 
 arrival of a guest who excite unwonted attention . a young woman , who 
 scarcely look her full age of thirty , small , slender , very simply and 
 elegantly dress , with something still girlish in her small irregular 
 feature and complexion of northern brilliancy , be conduct along the 
 gangway between the row of chair , and , as if she be the queen of the 
 entertainment , solemnly instal by the side of the Princess Rezzonico 
 in the first row . be it because her husband have call himself King of 
 England , or because her lover be the author of the play about to be 
 perform ? be it as it may , the Countess of Albany be the object of 
 universal curiosity , and the emotion which she display during the play 
 be a second and perhaps more interesting performance for the 
 scandal - love Romans . 


 while the ghost of these long dead man and woman , lady in voluminous 
 brocade skirt and diamond - cover bosom , burst out of the lace 
 and jewel of their stiff bodice , cardinal in trail scarlet robe 
 and bishop with well - powder hair contrast curiously with their 
 dominican or franciscan dress , roman noble all in the strange old - world 
 costume , with ruff and trunk hose and emblazon mantle , of the 
 Pope 's household and of the military order of Malta and Calatrava , 
 secular dandy in elaborately - embroider silk coat and waistcoat , 
 ecclesiastical dandy to the full as dapper with their heavy lace , 
 and abundant fob jewel and inevitable two watch on the sober black 
 of their clothe ; - while these ghost whom we have evoke in all 
 their finery   long since go to the bric- -brac shop   to fill the 
 theatre - hall of the spanish palace , sit and listen to the symphony 
 which Cimarosa himself have write for Antigone , sit and watch the 
 magnificent Duchess of Zagarolo , dress as Antigone in hoop and 
 stomacher and pile - up feather hair , and the red - haired eccentric 
 Piedmontese Count , the d'Albany 's lover , bellow the anger of Creon ; 
 let we try and sum up what the tragedy of Alfieri be for we people of 
 to - day , and what they must have be for those people of a hundred year 
 ago . 

 while scribble for mere pastime at his early play , Alfieri have feel 
 his mind illumine by a sort of double revelation : he would make his 
 name immortal , and he would create a new kind of tragedy . these two 
 half of a proposition , of which he appear never to have entertain a 
 single moment 's doubt , have originate at the same time and develop in 
 close connection : that he could be otherwise than an innovator be as 
 inconceivable to Alfieri as that he could be otherwise than a genius , 
 although , in reality , he be as far from be the one as from be 
 the other . the fact be that Alfieri feel in himself the power of 
 invent a style and of produce work which should answer to the 
 requirement of his own nature : consider himself as the sole audience , 
 he consider himself as the unique playwright . excessively limit in 
 his mental vision , and excessively strong in his mental muscle , it be 
 with his work as with his life : the ideal be so comparatively within 
 reach , and the will be so powerful , that one feel certain that he 
 nearly always succeed in behave in the way of which he approve , and 
 in write in the style which he admire . and the most extraordinary 
 part of the coincidence be , that as he happen to live in a time and 
 country which have entirely neglect the tragic stage , and consequently 
 have no habit or aspiration connect with it , his own desire with 
 reference to italian tragedy precede those of his fellow - countryman , 
 his own ideal be thrust upon they before they well know where they 
 be ; and his own nature and liking become the sole standard by which 
 he measure his work , his own satisfaction the only criterion by which 
 they could be judge . in order , therefore , to understand the nature of 
 Alfieri 's play , it be necessary , first of all , to understand what be 
 Alfieri 's innate liking and disliking in the domain of the drama . 
 before all other thing , Alfieri be not a poet : he lack all , or very 
 nearly all , the faculty which be really poetical . to begin with the 
 more gross and external one , he have no instinct for , no pleasure in , 
 metrical arrangement for their own sake ; he do not think nor invent in 
 verse , idea do not come to he on the wave of metre ; he think out , 
 he elaborately finish , every sentence in prose , and then translate 
 that prose into verse , as he might have translate   and in some 
 instance actually do translate   from a french version into an italian 
 one . moreover he be , to a degree which would have be surprising even 
 in a prose writer , deficient in that which constitute the intellectual 
 essence of poetry as metre constitute its material externality ; in 
 that tendency to see thing surround by , disguise in , a swarm , a 
 masquerade , of associated idea ; deficient in the power of suggest 
 image , of conceiving figure of speech ; in fancy , imagination , in the 
 metaphorical faculty , or whatever else we may choose to call it . nor do 
 he perceive or describe visible thing , visible effect , in their own 
 unmetaphorical shape and colour : not a line of description , not an 
 adjective can be find in his work except such as may be absolutely 
 indispensable for topographical or similar intelligibility ; Alfieri 
 obviously care as little for beautiful sight as for beautiful sound . 
 this be the case , everything that we might call distinctly poetical , 
 all those thing which be precious to we in Shakespeare , or Marlowe , 
 or Webster , in Goethe or Schiller , nay , even , occur at interval , 
 in Racine himself , at least as much as mere psychology or oratory or 
 pathos , appear to Alfieri in the light of mere meretricious gewgaw , 
 which take away from the interest of dramatic action without afford 
 he any satisfaction in return . as it be with metre and metaphor and 
 description , so it be also with the indefinable something which we call 
 lyric quality : the something which sing to our soul , and which send a 
 thrill of delight through our nerve or a gust of emotion across our 
 nature in the same direct way as do the note of certain voice , the 
 phrase of certain piece of music : instantaneously , unreasoningly and 
 unerringly . of this Alfieri have little , so little that we may also say 
 that he have nothing ; the presence of this quality be evidently 
 unnoticed by he and unappreciated . so much for the absolutely poetical 
 quality . of what I may call the prose quality of a playwright , only 
 a certain number appeal to Alfieri , and only a certain number be 
 possess by he . in a time when the novel be begin to become a 
 psychological study more minute than any stage play could ever be , 
 Alfieri be only very moderately interested in the subtle analysis or 
 representation of character and state of mind ; the fine touch which 
 bring home a person or a situation do not attract his attention ; nor 
 be he trouble by consideration concern the probability of a 
 give word or word be speak at a particular moment and by a 
 particular man or woman : realism have no meaning for he . as it be 
 with intellectual conception , so be it also with instructive sympathy : 
 Alfieri never subtly analyse the anatomy of individual nature , nor 
 do he unconsciously mimic its action and tone ; what most of we mean 
 by pathos do not appeal to he . neither metrical nor imaginative 
 pleasurableness , nor descriptive charm , nor lyric poignancy , nor 
 psychological analysis or intention enter , therefore , into Alfieri 's 
 conception of a desirable tragedy , any more than any of these thing 
 fall within the range of his special talent ; for , we must always bear 
 in mind that with this man , whose feeling and desire be in such 
 constant action and reaction , with this man whose will impose his 
 intellectual notion on his feeling , and his emotional tendency on 
 his thought , the thing which he enjoy be always as the concave to the 
 convex of the thing which he produce . but although Alfieri be not a 
 poet , and be not even a potential novel writer , he be , in a sense , 
 essentially a dramatist ; though even here we must distinguish and 
 diminish . Alfieri be not a man who care for rapid action or for 
 intricate plot : he never feel the small inclination to violate the 
 old tradition of the pseudo - classic stage by those thrilling scene 
 or sight which have to be describe and not show , nor by those 
 complication of interest which require year for an action instead of 
 the orthodox twenty - four hour . 

 he be perfectly satisfied with the no - place , no - where - with the vague 
 temple , or palace hall , or public square where , as in the country of the 
 abstract , the action of pseudo - classic tragedy always take place , or , 
 more properly speak , the talking of pseudo - classic tragedy always 
 go on ; he be perfectly satisfied with send in a servant or a 
 messenger to inform the public of a murder or suicide commit behind 
 the scene ; he be perfectly satisfied with take up a story , so to 
 speak , at the eleventh hour , without trace it to its original cause 
 or develop it through its various phase . in such matter Alfieri be 
 as undramatic as Corneille or Racine . nevertheless Alfieri have a 
 distinct dramatic sense : an intense poseur himself , enjoy nothing 
 so much as work himself up to produce a give effect upon his own 
 mind or upon other , he have an extraordinary instinct for the theatrical , 
 for the moral attitude which may be strike so as to be effective , and 
 for the arrangement of subordinate part so that this attitude surprise 
 and move the audience . the moral attitude , the psychological gesture , 
 which thus become the main interest of Alfieri 's play , be , as might be 
 expect from such a man , nearly always his own moral attitude , his own 
 psychological gesture ; he himself , his uncompromising , unhesitate , 
 unflinche , curt and emphatic nature , be always the hero or heroine 
 of the play , however much the situation , the incident , the other 
 characteristic may vary . Antigone be generous and tender , Creon be 
 inhuman in all save paternal feeling , Saul be a suspicious madman , 
 Agamemnon a just and confide hero , Clytæmnestra be sinful and 
 self - sophisticate , Virginia pure and open - minded ; yet all these 
 different people , despite all their difference , speak and act as 
 Alfieri would speak and act , could he , without lose his peculiar 
 characteristic , adopt for the moment vice or virtue which would 
 become quite secondary matter by the side of his essential quality of 
 pride , narrowness , decision , violence , and self - importance . whether he 
 paint his face into a smile or a scowl , whether he put on the blond wig 
 of innocence , or the black wig of villainy , the man 's movement and 
 gesture , the tone of his voice , the accent of his word , the length of 
 his sentence , be always the same : so much so that in one play there 
 may be two or three Alfieris , good and bad , Alfieris turn perfectly 
 virtuous or perfectly vicious ; but anything that be not an Alfieri in 
 some tolerably transparent disguise , be sure to be a puppet , a lay 
 figure with as few joint as possible , just able to stretch out its arm 
 and clap they to its side , but dangle suspend between heaven and 
 earth . 

 the attitude and the gesture , which be the thing for whose sake the 
 play exist , be , as I have say , the attitude and gesture of Alfieri . 
 but the moral attitude and gesture of Alfieri happen to be just those 
 which be rarest in the eighteenth century in all country , and more 
 especially rare in Italy ; and they be the moral attitude and gesture 
 which the eighteenth century absolutely require to become the nineteenth , 
 and which the Italy of Peter Leopold and Pius VI . and metastasio 
 and Goldoni absolutely require to become the Italy of Mazzini and 
 Garibaldi , the Italy of Foscolo and Leopardi : they be the attitude and 
 the gesture of single - mindedness , haughtiness , indifference to one 's 
 own comfort and one 's neighbour ' opinion , the attitude and gesture 
 of manliness , of strength , if you will , of heroism . to have write 
 tragedy whose whole value depend upon the striking exhibition of 
 these quality ; and to have make this exhibition interesting , nay , 
 fascinating to the very people , to the amiable , humane , indifferent , 
 lie , feeble - spirited Italians of the latter eighteenth century , till 
 these very man be ashamed of what they have hitherto be ; to stamp the 
 new generation with the clear - cut die of his own strong character ; this 
 be the reality of the mission which Alfieri have feel within himself : a 
 reality which will be remember when his play shall have long cease 
 to be act , and shall long have cease to be read . Alfieri imagine 
 himself to be a great poetic genius , and a great dramatic innovator : 
 he scorn with loathe the work of Corneille , of Racine , and of 
 Voltaire , all immeasurably more valuable as poetry and drama than his 
 own ; he hat the work of Metastasio , a poet and a playwright by the 
 divine right of genius ; he refuse to read Shakespeare , lest Shakespeare 
 should spoil the perfection of his own conception . he slave for month 
 and year perfect each of his play , recast the action and 
 curtail the dialogue and polish the verse ; yet the action be 
 always heavy , the dialogue unnatural to the last degree , the verse 
 unpoetical . but all this extraordinary self - sufficiency be not a 
 delusion , all this extraordinary labour be not a waste : Alfieri , who 
 never have a single poetical thought , nor a single art - revolutionise 
 notion , be yet a great genius and a great innovator , inasmuch as he 
 first mould in his own image the italian patriot of the nineteenth 
 century . his use consist in his mere existence among man so different 
 from himself ; and his drama , his elaborately construct and curtail 
 and correct drama , be , so to speak , a system of mirror by which 
 the image of this strange new - fangled personality might be flash 
 everywhere into the soul of his contemporary . to perceive the 
 moral attitude and gesture specially characteristic of himself , to 
 artificially correct and improve and isolate they in his own reality , 
 and then to multiply their likeness for all the world ; to know himself 
 to be Alfieri , to make himself up as Alfieri , and to write play whereof 
 the hero and heroine be mere repetition of Alfieri ; such be the 
 mission of this powerful and spontaneous nature , of this self - conscious 
 and self - manipulate poseur . 

 the success of that performance of Antigone on the amateur stage 
 in the spanish palace be very great . a young man , half lay , half 
 ecclesiastic , a dubious sort of poet , secretary , factotum , accustom to 
 write not the most sincere poetry , and to execute , perhaps , not the most 
 creditable errand , of the Pope 's dubious nephew , Duke Braschi - a young 
 man name Vincenzo Monti , be present at this performance , or one 
 of the succeed one ; and from that moment become the author of the 
 revolutionary tragedy of Aristodemo , the potential author of that 
 famous ode on the battle of Marengo , one of the forerunner of new 
 Italy . Nay , even when , some few month later , there die at Vienna the 
 old Abate Metastasio , and his death bring home to a rather forgetful 
 world what a poet and what a dramatist that old Metastasio have be ; 
 even then , an intimate friend of the dead man , a worldly priest , a quasi 
 prelate , the Abate Taruffi , could find no well wind up for the 
 funeral oration , deliver before all the pedant and prig and fop and 
 spy of pontifical Rome assemble in the room of the arcadian academy , 
 than to point to Count Vittorio Alfieri , and prophesy that Metastasio 
 have find a successor great than himself .

 separation .

 CHAPTER XII . 

 COLMAR . 


 " on the 17th August 1784 , at eight in the morning , at the inn of the 
  two Keys , Colmar , I meet she , and remain speechless from excess of 
 joy . " so run an annotation of Alfieri on the margin of one of his 
 lyric . 

 the hour of liberty and happiness have come for Alfieri and Mme . 
 d'Albany ; soon by far than they expect , and soon , we may think , 
 than they deserve . liberty and happiness , however , not in the face 
 of the law . Charles Edward be still alive ; but , press by King 
 Gustavus III . of Sweden , whom he contrive to wheedle out of some most 
 unnecessary money , he have consent to a legal separation from his 
 fugitive wife ; as a result of which the Countess of Albany , renounce 
 all money supply from the stuart , and subsist entirely upon a 
 share of the two pension , French and Papal , grant to her husband , 
 be permit to spend a portion of the year wheresoever she please , 
 provide she return for awhile to show herself in the Papal States . 
 on hear the unexpected news , Alfieri , who be cross the Apennines 
 of Modena with fourteen horse that he have be to buy in England , be 
 seize with a violent temptation to send his caravan along the main 
 road , and gallop by cross - path to meet the Countess , who be cross 
 the Apennines of Bologna on her way from Rome to the bath of Baden in 
 Switzerland . the thought of her honour and safety restrain he , and he 
 push on moodily to Siena . but , as on a previous occasion , his stern 
 resolution not to seek his lady soon give way ; and two month later 
 follow that meeting at the two Keys at Colmar on the Rhine . 

 for the first time in those seven long year of platonic passion , 
 Alfieri and Mme . d'Albany find themselves settle beneath the same 
 roof . to the mind of this italian man , and this half - french , half - german 
 woman of the eighteenth century , for whom marriage be one of the 
 sacrament of a religion in which they wholly disbelieve , and one 
 of the institution of a society which alleviate it with universal 
 adultery ; to Alfieri and Mme . d'Albany the legal separation from Charles 
 Edward Stuart be equivalent to a divorce . the Pretender could no long 
 prescribe any line of conduct to his wife ; she be free to live where 
 and with whom she choose ; and if she be not free to marry , the idea , 
 the wish for marriage , probably never cross the brain of these two 
 platonic lover of seven year ' standing . marriage be a social contract 
 between people who wish to obtain each other 's money and title and 
 land - who wish to have heir . Alfieri , who have make over all his 
 property to his sister , and the Countess , who live on a pension , have no 
 money or title or land to throw together ; and they certainly neither 
 of they , the man live entirely for his work , the woman live entirely 
 for the man , have the small desire to have child , heir to nothing 
 at all . what injury could their live together now do to Charles Edward , 
 who have relinquish all his husband 's right ? none , evidently . on the 
 other hand , what harm could their live together do to their own honour 
 or happiness , now that they have have seven year ' experience that only 
 death could extinguish their affection ? none , again evidently . and as to 
 harm to the institution of society , what be those institution , and 
 what be their value , that they should be respect ? such , could we 
 have question they , would have be the answer of Alfieri and the 
 Countess . that they be set an example to other less pure in mind , 
 less exceptional in position ; that they be make it more difficult 
 for marriage to be reorganise on a more rational plan , by show man 
 and woman a something that might do instead of rationally organise 
 marriage ; that they be , in short , prevent the law from be 
 rectify , by take the law into their own hand : such thought could 
 not enter into the mind of continental of the eighteenth century , 
 people for whom the great Revolution , Romanticism , and the new view 
 of society which grow out of both , be still in the future . that a 
 punishment should await they , that as time go on and youthful passion 
 diminish , their life should be barren and silent and cold for want of 
 all those thing : child , legal bond , social recognition , by which 
 their union should fall short of a real marriage ; this they could never 
 anticipate . 

 for the moment , unite in the " excessively clean and comfortable " little 
 château , rent by Madame d'Albany at a short distance from Colmar ; 
 rid and drive about in the lovely Rhine country ; the Countess deep 
 in her reading again , Alfieri deep once more in his writing ; together , 
 above all , after so many month of separation : they seem perfectly 
 happy . so happy that it seem as if a misfortune must come to restore 
 the natural balance of thing ; and the misfortune come , in the sudden 
 news of the death of poor Francesco Gori . a sense as of guiltiness at 
 have half forget that thoughtful and gentle friend in the first 
 flush of their happiness , seem to have come over they . 

 " o God , " write Alfieri to Gori 's friend Bianchi at Siena , " I do not   know 
 what I shall do . I always see he and speak to he , and every small 
 word and thought and gesture of his return to my mind , and stab my 
 heart . I do not feel very sorry for he : he care little for life for 
 its own sake , and the life which he be force to lead be too far below 
 his great soul , and the goodness and tenderness of his heart , and the 
 nobility of his noble scornfulness . the person dear to I of any , 
 and immediately next to whom I love Checco   Gori   most , know and 
 appreciate he and be not to be console for such a loss . I tell he 
 already last July , so many , many time , that he be not well , that he 
 be grow visibly thin day by day . oh ! I ought never to have leave 
 he in this state . " 

 a letter , this one on Gori 's death , which may induce we to forgive the 
 letter of Alfieri of which we have see a reflection in those of Mme . 
 d'Albany : the passionate grief for the lost friend make we feel that 
 there be something noble in the possibility of even the morbid grief at 
 the lost mistress . more touching still , bring home what each of we , 
 alas ! must have feel in those long , dull grief for one who be not

 the death of Gori seem the only circumstance which diminish the

 suppose to be live apart from Alfieri , the Countess could not ,

 strange word , and which , come from a man cynical and truthful as

 " it grieve I dreadfully to think that I should die , leave my lady ,

 Alfieri recover . but with that illness end , I think , the period of

 CHAPTER XIII . 

 RUE DE BOURGOYNE .

 one see throughout all his autobiography and letter that Alfieri draw

 the Countess of Albany do not fit into the first ideal ; nor , for the

 CHAPTER XIV 

 before the storm . 


 the well - bear and amusing people of the end of the eighteenth and

 to all these great and illustrious people the Countess of Albany - I have 
 almost say the Queen of England - introduce her " incomparable friend " 
  style then in vogue   Count Vittorio Alfieri ; and all of they doubtless

 Alfieri be not . ever since his childhood , when a french dancing - master

 Alfieri , with his fault and his virtue , be specially construct , if 
 I may use the expression , to ignore all the good point , and to feel 
 with hysterical sensitiveness all the bad one , of the french nation ; 
 and more especially of the french nation of the pre - revolutionary and 
 revolutionary era . Alfieri 's reality and Alfieri 's ideal be austerity , 
 inflexibility , pride and contemptuousness of character , coldness , 
 roughness , decision of manner , curtness , reticence , and absolute 
 truthfulness of speech ; above all , no consideration for other folk ' 
 liking and disliking , no mercy for their foible . his ideal , even more 
 so than the ideal of other idealising mind , be the mere outcome of 
 himself ; it contain his fault as well as his virtue . now all that 
 fall short of , or go beyond , his ideal - that be to say , himself - be 
 abomination in Alfieri 's eye . consequently France and the French , 
 all the nobility , the wit , the sentiment , the warm - heartedness , the 
 enthusiasm , the wide - mindedness , the childishness , the frivolity , the 
 instability , the disrespectfulness , the sentimentality , the high 
 falutinism , the superficiality , the looseness of principle , everything 
 that make up the greatness and littleness of the France of the end of 
 last century , everything which will make up the greatness and littleness 
 of France , the glory and weakness which the world must love , to the 
 end of time ; all these thing be abhorrent to Alfieri ; and Alfieri , 
 when once he dislike a person or a thing , justly or unjustly , could 
 only increase but never diminish his dislike . let we look at this

 run to injustice , a little close ; it will help we to understand the 
  Misogallo , the extraordinary apostasy which , quite unconsciously , 
 Alfieri be later to commit towards the principle of freedom . Alfieri , 
 intensely italian , if mediæval and peasant Italy may give we the 
 italian type , in a certain silent or rather inarticulate violence of 
 temper - violence which roar and yell and stab and strangle , but 
 which never talk , and much less argue - could not endure the particular 
 sort of excitement which surround he in France ; excitement mainly 
 cerebral , heroism or villainy result , but only as the outcome of 
 argument and definition of principle and of that mixture of logic and

 mind , he be not an eloquent man ; above all , he be not a witty man ; his 
 satirical effort be so many blow upon an opponent 's head ; they be 
 almost physical brutality ; there be nothing clever or funny about 
 they . in such a society as this parisian society of the year ' 87 , ' 88 , 
 ' 89 , ' 90 , he must have be at a continual disadvantage ; and at a

 any remarkable piece of Alfierism which would have move Italy to

 man 's head , could only increase . to feel himself at a disadvantage on

 not impress , must have be most intolerable to a man as vain and 
 self - conscious as Alfieri , and to this be add the sense that , 
 from mere ignorance of the language   the language whose nobility , as 
 contrast with the " low , plebeian , nasal disgustingness " of French , he 
 so often descanted on   in which he write , it be quite impossible for 
 these people to be reduce to their right place and right mind by the 
 crush superiority of his dramatic genius . he , who hunger and 
 thirst for glory , what glory could he hope for among all these monkey 
 of Frenchmen , jabber and gesticulate about their States - General , 
 their Montgolfier , their St. Pierre , their Condorcet , their Parny , their 
 Necker , who have not even the decent feeling to know italian , and who 
 bow and smile and doubtless mix he up with Metastasio and Goldoni 
 when introduce by the Countess to so odd a piece of provincialism as an 
 italian poet . " do Monsieur write comedy or tragedy ? " one fancy
